## 6. Security Architecture

This chapter is organized by security-control category. The architecture section avoids artificial control IDs and finding-ID columns in overview tables. Findings are listed only where the affected control is described.

_[§6](#6-security-architecture) schema v2 (13-section control-category layout). Cataloged controls: 25 total - 2 adequate, 10 partial, 10 weak, 0 unsafe, 3 missing. Linked threats: 68._

**How to read the verdicts.** Every control category (and every sub-control below it) carries exactly one status. The two red verdicts do **not** mean the same thing - this is the distinction that decides what you have to do about a finding:

| Status | Meaning | What it asks of you |
|----------|------------------------------------|------------------------|
| 🟢 Adequate | Control is present and sound | Nothing - keep it |
| 🟡 Partial | Present, but with meaningful gaps | Close the gap |
| 🟠 Weak | Present, but has exploitable gaps | Strengthen it |
| 🔴 Unsafe | **Present and relied upon, but defeated /<br/>trivially bypassable** | **Fix the existing control** |
| 🔴 Missing | **Control was never built** | **Add the control** |
| - | Not applicable to this codebase | - |

So "🔴 Unsafe" on a control category does *not* mean the control is absent - it means the control exists but does not hold (e.g. an `MD5` password hash, a raw-SQL query path, a hardcoded signing key). "🔴 Missing" is reserved for controls that were never built (e.g. no Content-Security-Policy header).

### 6.1 Security Control Overview

<!-- §6.1 MECHANICAL-FROZEN — DO NOT EDIT (overview table is pregenerator-owned) -->

| Control category | Verdict | Main reason |
|----------------------|---------|------------------------------------|
| [6.2 Identity and Authentication Controls](#62-identity-and-authentication-controls) | 🟠 Weak | 8 routed findings; catalogued controls are<br/>weak (e.g. Password-Based Authentication,<br/>OAuth2 / Social Login). |
| [6.3 Session and Token Controls](#63-session-and-token-controls) | 🟠 Weak | 2 routed findings; catalogued controls are<br/>weak (e.g. JWT Issuance and Validation,<br/>Token Storage). |
| [6.4 Authorization Controls](#64-authorization-controls) | 🟠 Weak | 8 routed findings; catalogued controls are<br/>weak (e.g. Role-Based Access Control,<br/>Client-Side Route Guards). |
| [6.5 Query Construction and Data Access Controls](#65-query-construction-and-data-access-controls) | 🟠 Weak | 3 routed findings; catalogued controls are<br/>weak (e.g. ORM Parameterization). |
| [6.6 Input Boundary Validation Controls](#66-input-boundary-validation-controls) | 🟠 Weak | 6 routed findings; catalogued controls are<br/>weak (e.g. Server-Side Input Validation). |
| [6.7 Output Encoding and Rendering Controls](#67-output-encoding-and-rendering-controls) | 🟠 Weak | 1 routed finding; catalogued controls are<br/>weak (e.g. Angular Template Security). |
| [6.8 Browser and Cross-Origin Controls](#68-browser-and-cross-origin-controls) | 🔴 Missing | Required controls not in place (e.g. Content<br/>Security Policy, CORS Configuration). |
| [6.9 Cryptography Secrets and Data Protection](#69-cryptography-secrets-and-data-protection) | 🟠 Weak | 7 routed findings; catalogued controls are<br/>weak (e.g. Password Hashing, Secret<br/>Management). |
| [6.10 File Parser and Outbound Request Controls](#610-file-parser-and-outbound-request-controls) | 🟠 Weak | 7 routed findings; catalogued controls are<br/>weak (e.g. File Upload Security, XML / YAML<br/>Parsing). |
| [6.11 Operations Runtime and Supply Chain Controls](#611-operations-runtime-and-supply-chain-controls) | 🔴 Missing | 9 routed findings; required controls not in<br/>place (e.g. Container Security, CI/CD Supply<br/>Chain Security). |
| [6.12 Real-time and Not Applicable Controls](#612-real-time-and-not-applicable-controls) | 🟠 Weak | 0 routed findings; catalogued controls are<br/>weak (e.g. Socket\.IO Authentication, LLM /<br/>AI Exposure Controls). |
| [6.13 Defense-in-Depth Summary](#613-defense-in-depth-summary) | - | No controls or findings routed to this<br/>category. |

<!-- §6.1 MECHANICAL-FROZEN END -->

### 6.2 Identity and Authentication Controls


**Systemic weaknesses:** [W-004](#w-004)
**Verdict:** 🟠 Weak

<!-- The line below is mechanically derived from the controls table — LLM must not re-author it. -->
**Controls covered:**

- [6.2.1 Password-Based Authentication](#621-password-based-authentication)
- [6.2.2 OAuth2 / Social Login](#622-oauth2--social-login)
- [6.2.3 TOTP Two-Factor Authentication](#623-totp-two-factor-authentication)

**Implemented controls:** Password-based login via `routes/login.ts`, TOTP enrollment and verification via `routes/totp.ts`, OAuth frontend adapter via `oauth.component.ts`, user registration auto-CRUD via `server.ts`.

**Assessment:** The authentication boundary is compromised at its foundation: `routes/login.ts:34` concatenates `req.body.email` directly into raw SQL rather than using a parameterized query, allowing authentication bypass without a password. The RSA private key used for JWT issuance is hardcoded at `lib/insecurity.ts:21`, meaning any clone of the repository can forge valid session tokens. The OAuth adapter derives a predictable local password from the email claim rather than issuing an IdP-bound credential. Each successful flow above terminates in the server issuing a session token; the signing, validation, propagation, storage, and lifecycle of that token are described in [§6.3 Session and Token Controls](#63-session-and-token-controls).

<!-- §6.2 AUTH-MECHANISMS-FROZEN — deterministic inventory, pregenerator-owned. DO NOT EDIT. -->
**Authentication mechanisms (at a glance).** Every authentication mechanism detected on the application, its effective status, where it is assessed, and its linked findings. Controls are catalogued by domain, so JWT/session handling is assessed under [§6.3 Session and Token Controls](#63-session-and-token-controls) and password hashing under [§6.9 Cryptography Secrets and Data Protection](#69-cryptography-secrets-and-data-protection).

| Mechanism | Status | Assessed in | Findings |
|----------------------|----------|-----------|------------------------------------------------|
| User registration | 🔴 Critical | [§6.2](#62-identity-and-authentication-controls) | 🔴 [F-014](#f-014) — Mass Assignment via finale-rest Auto-CRUD on User Model (`server.ts:501`)<br/>🔴 [F-015](#f-015) — Mass assignment allows self-registration as admin via role field<br/>🟠 [F-046](#f-046) — No Rate Limiting or Lockout on Login and Registration Endpoints |
| Password login | 🟡 Partial | [§6.2](#62-identity-and-authentication-controls) | 🟠 [F-048](#f-048) — Missing rate limiting on login endpoint enables credential stuffing and brute-f… |
| Password reset / change | 🟠 High | [§6.2](#62-identity-and-authentication-controls) | 🟠 [F-016](#f-016) — Security-Question Password Reset Without Rate Limiting (`resetPassword.ts:17`)<br/>🟠 [F-018](#f-018) — Password reset relies solely on security question with OSINT-guessable answers |
| Password storage (hashing) | 🟢 Adequate | [§6.9](#69-cryptography-secrets-and-data-protection) | [W-007](#w-007) — Security-sensitive data uses weak cryptographic primitives |
| JWT / bearer-token session | 🟠 Weak | [§6.3](#63-session-and-token-controls) | 🟠 [F-001](#f-001) — JWT Session Token Written to localStorage (`oauth.component.ts:51`)<br/>[W-007](#w-007) — Security-sensitive data uses weak cryptographic primitives<br/>🔴 [F-007](#f-007) — Insecure JWT Verification<br/>🟠 [F-017](#f-017) — Express-jwt 0.1.3 Critically Outdated JWT Middleware (`insecurity.ts:10`)<br/>🟠 [F-052](#f-052) — No Server-Side JWT Revocation Mechanism (`insecurity.ts:70`) |
| Session-token storage | 🟠 Weak | [§6.3](#63-session-and-token-controls) | 🟠 [F-001](#f-001) — JWT Session Token Written to localStorage (`oauth.component.ts:51`)<br/>🟠 [F-033](#f-033) — TOTP Intermediate Token Stored in localStorage (`login.component.ts:120`)<br/>🟠 [F-042](#f-042) — TOTP Pre-Authentication Token Stored in localStorage (`login.component.ts:120`) |
| Multi-factor authentication (TOTP / 2FA) | 🟡 Partial | [§6.2](#62-identity-and-authentication-controls) | 🟠 [F-033](#f-033) — TOTP Intermediate Token Stored in localStorage (`login.component.ts:120`)<br/>🟠 [F-042](#f-042) — TOTP Pre-Authentication Token Stored in localStorage (`login.component.ts:120`)<br/>🟡 [F-057](#f-057) — Account Existence and 2FA Enrollment Disclosed via Differential Login Response |
| OAuth / OIDC federated login | 🟡 Partial | [§6.2](#62-identity-and-authentication-controls) | 🟠 [F-001](#f-001) — JWT Session Token Written to localStorage (`oauth.component.ts:51`)<br/>[W-003](#w-003) — Secrets are committed to source instead of a managed store<br/>🔴 [F-009](#f-009) — Derived Credential from OAuth Email Claim (`oauth.component.ts:30`)<br/>🟠 [F-019](#f-019) — OAuth Implicit Flow Without State Parameter (`login.component.ts:148`) |

<!-- §6.2 AUTH-MECHANISMS-FROZEN END -->

<a id="password-based-authentication"></a>
#### 6.2.1 Password-Based Authentication

**Status:** 🟡 Partial - login is present and functional but the SQL query path allows bypass without a valid password, and the reset flow relies solely on an OSINT-guessable security question.

`routes/login.ts` handles credential submission. A registered user supplies an email and password; the server looks up the account and compares the submitted hash against the stored value in the `Users` table. Password reset is handled separately at `routes/resetPassword.ts` via a security-question challenge.

The diagram shows the intended password-login path from credential submission through session-token issuance:

```mermaid
sequenceDiagram
    autonumber
    actor User
    participant SPA as Angular SPA
    participant API as Express Backend
    participant DB
    Note over DB: SQLite (Users table)
    participant JWT as JWT Issuer

    User->>SPA: Submit email and password
    SPA->>API: POST /rest/user/login
    API->>DB: SELECT where email = '...'
    DB-->>API: User row with password hash
    API->>API: Compare submitted hash
    API->>JWT: Sign RS256 token with lib/insecurity.ts key
    JWT-->>API: Signed token
    API-->>SPA: 200 OK with token and basketId
    SPA->>SPA: localStorage.setItem("token", ...)
```

**Security assessment**

Two independent weaknesses break the login boundary:

- `routes/login.ts:34` concatenates `req.body.email` into a raw SQL string passed to `models.sequelize.query()`. Submitting `' OR '1'='1` as the email bypasses password verification and returns the first row - the seeded admin account.
- `routes/resetPassword.ts:17` resets a password on a correct security-question answer with no rate limiting, allowing an attacker to enumerate answers for any known email address using publicly available (OSINT) information.

**Relevant findings**

- 🔴 [F-007 — Insecure JWT Verification](#f-007) — insecure JWT verification at `lib/insecurity.ts:52` means the session token issued after login carries no pinned algorithm, so a forged token can traverse the same middleware.
- 🔴 [F-008 — Unsalted MD5 Password Hashing](#f-008) — unsalted MD5 password storage at `models/user.ts:76` means a dump obtained via the SQL injection above yields recoverable credentials without a rainbow table.
- 🟠 [F-016 — Security-Question Password Reset Without Rate Limiting](#f-016) — security-question password reset at `routes/resetPassword.ts:17` provides no rate limiting or token-based proof of ownership, enabling account takeover for any user with a guessable answer.

<a id="oauth2-social-login"></a>
#### 6.2.2 OAuth2 / Social Login

**Status:** 🟡 Partial - the OAuth adapter terminates in local password login, so the session inherits all local-login weaknesses; no state parameter prevents CSRF against the OAuth redirect.

`oauth.component.ts` implements federated login as a frontend-only adapter. It reads an access token from the OAuth redirect URL, calls the Google userinfo endpoint, derives a local password from the returned email address at `oauth.component.ts:30`, and then calls `/rest/user/login` with that derived credential. No server-side authorization-code flow or IdP-issued token validation occurs.

The diagram shows how the OAuth adapter enters the local login path:

```mermaid
sequenceDiagram
    autonumber
    actor User
    participant SPA
    Note over SPA: OAuth Component
    participant Google
    Note over Google: Google UserInfo API
    participant API
    Note over API: Local User and Login API
    participant JWT
    Note over JWT: JWT Issuer

    User->>SPA: Return from OAuth redirect with access token
    SPA->>Google: GET userinfo with access token
    Google-->>SPA: Email address
    SPA->>API: POST /rest/user (create if absent)
    SPA->>API: POST /rest/user/login with derived password
    API->>JWT: Sign RS256 session token
    API-->>SPA: Token and basketId
    SPA->>SPA: localStorage.setItem("token", ...)
```

**Security assessment**

This is a frontend OAuth identity adapter, not a server-side OAuth/OIDC control. Three structural weaknesses follow from the adapter design:

- `oauth.component.ts:30` derives the local password from the email claim using a deterministic algorithm exposed in the repository, so anyone who knows a user's email and clones the repo can compute their local password and authenticate without an OAuth token.
- `login.component.ts:148` initiates the OAuth redirect without a `state` parameter, making the flow vulnerable to CSRF: an attacker can force a victim to complete a login under the attacker's Google identity.
- The resulting session JWT inherits the same signing-key and algorithm weaknesses as password-based login (see [§6.3](#63-session-and-token-controls)).

**Relevant findings**

- 🔴 [F-007 — Insecure JWT Verification](#f-007) — insecure JWT verification affects the session token produced by the OAuth login path, since the token is issued and verified by the same broken middleware as password logins.
- 🔴 [F-008 — Unsalted MD5 Password Hashing](#f-008) — the local account created for an OAuth user stores the derived password as unsalted MD5, so a database dump exposes those derived credentials alongside native ones.
- 🟠 [F-016 — Security-Question Password Reset Without Rate Limiting](#f-016) — OAuth-linked accounts share the same password-reset path at `routes/resetPassword.ts`, inheriting the security-question weakness that can be exploited to replace the derived password.

<a id="totp-two-factor-authentication"></a>
#### 6.2.3 TOTP Two-Factor Authentication

**Status:** 🟡 Partial - TOTP enrollment is available and functional but the control is opt-in only, intermediate tokens are written to localStorage, and the login response discloses 2FA enrollment status before the TOTP step completes.

TOTP provides a possession-factor second step for opted-in users. After the first-factor password check passes at `routes/login.ts`, the server issues an intermediate token used to gate the TOTP verification step. `login.component.ts:120` stores this token in `localStorage` and reads it back when the user submits their TOTP code.

The diagram shows the intended two-step login path including TOTP verification:

```mermaid
sequenceDiagram
    autonumber
    actor User
    participant SPA as Angular SPA
    participant API as Express Backend
    participant JWT as JWT Issuer

    User->>SPA: Submit email and password
    SPA->>API: POST /rest/user/login
    API-->>SPA: 200 with totp_token (2FA required)
    SPA->>SPA: localStorage.setItem("totp_token", ...)
    User->>SPA: Submit TOTP code
    SPA->>API: POST /rest/2fa/verify with code and totp_token
    API->>API: Verify TOTP code against secret
    API->>JWT: Sign RS256 session token
    API-->>SPA: 200 with session token
    SPA->>SPA: localStorage.setItem("token", ...)
```

**Security assessment**

TOTP adds a possession factor but three gaps limit its effectiveness:

- `login.component.ts:120` writes both the intermediate TOTP token and the pre-authentication token to `localStorage`, which is readable by any same-origin script - an XSS finding (🔴 [F-029 — Cross-Site Scripting](#f-029)) provides the path to steal these tokens before the second factor completes.
- `routes/login.ts:38` returns a differentiable response when a password is correct and 2FA is enrolled versus when the password is wrong, disclosing account existence and 2FA enrollment to an unauthenticated attacker.
- TOTP enrollment is optional and not enforced for admin-role accounts, so the highest-privilege accounts may operate with only the first factor.

**Relevant findings**

- 🔴 [F-007 — Insecure JWT Verification](#f-007) — insecure JWT verification means the final session token issued after TOTP passes is not protected by a pinned algorithm, so the possession-factor check does not raise the overall session assurance level.
- 🔴 [F-008 — Unsalted MD5 Password Hashing](#f-008) — the knowledge-factor password verified before TOTP is stored as unsalted MD5, so TOTP provides a second factor over a first factor that is already recoverable from a database dump.
- 🟠 [F-016 — Security-Question Password Reset Without Rate Limiting](#f-016) — password reset bypasses TOTP entirely: a successful security-question reset issues a new password without re-enrolling or re-verifying the second factor.

### 6.3 Session and Token Controls

**Verdict:** 🟠 Weak

<!-- The line below is mechanically derived from the controls table — LLM must not re-author it. -->
**Controls covered:**

- [6.3.1 JWT Issuance and Validation](#631-jwt-issuance-and-validation)
- [6.3.2 Token Storage](#632-token-storage)

**Implemented controls:** `RS256` JWT signing via `lib/insecurity.ts`, `express-jwt@0.1.3` validation middleware mounted on protected routes, `localStorage`-based token persistence in the browser.

**Assessment:** This application uses a single locally-signed token format (commonly called JWT) for every authenticated session, regardless of the login flow in [§6.2](#62-identity-and-authentication-controls) that established it. The sub-sections below trace one token through its lifecycle: signing on issuance, validation on every protected request, storage in the browser, manual revocation, and time-based expiry.

<a id="jwt-issuance-and-validation"></a>
#### 6.3.1 JWT Issuance and Validation

**Status:** 🟠 Weak - `RS256` is configured but `express-jwt@0.1.3` accepts `alg:none`, and the private key is committed to the repository, defeating both the signing and verification controls.

`lib/insecurity.ts` signs every session JWT with an RSA private key on login and issues the token as a JSON response body. All protected API routes mount `express-jwt@0.1.3` middleware (`lib/insecurity.ts:52`) to verify the token signature before the route handler runs.

The diagram shows the token-signing path on login and the verification path on a protected request:

```mermaid
sequenceDiagram
    autonumber
    actor User
    participant API as Express Backend
    participant Signer
    Note over Signer: JWT Issuer (lib/insecurity.ts)
    participant Verifier
    Note over Verifier: express-jwt middleware

    User->>API: POST /rest/user/login (credentials)
    API->>Signer: sign(payload, hardcodedPrivateKey, RS256)
    Signer-->>API: Signed JWT
    API-->>User: 200 OK with JWT

    User->>Verifier: GET /api/... with Authorization header
    Verifier->>Verifier: Decode header - alg field trusted from token
    Verifier-->>API: req.user populated
    API-->>User: Protected resource
```

**Security assessment**

Two independent weaknesses make the signing and verification controls ineffective:

- `lib/insecurity.ts:21` stores the RSA private key as a hardcoded string literal in the public repository. Any clone of the repository can sign arbitrary payloads as any user with `role:admin`.
- `lib/insecurity.ts:52` configures `express-jwt@0.1.3` without an `algorithms` allowlist. An attacker can craft a token with `"alg":"none"` in the header, removing the signature entirely, and the middleware accepts it as valid.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟠 [F-001 — JWT Session Token Written to localStorage](#f-001) — the JWT written to `localStorage` at `oauth.component.ts:51` is the session artifact whose signing and verification are assessed here; compromising the signing key directly yields a valid token without touching localStorage.
- 🟠 [F-052 — No Server-Side JWT Revocation Mechanism](#f-052) — no server-side revocation mechanism exists at `lib/insecurity.ts:70`, so a forged or stolen token remains valid until the hardcoded expiry time elapses.

<a id="token-storage"></a>
#### 6.3.2 Token Storage

**Status:** 🟠 Weak - session JWTs are written to browser `localStorage`, accessible to any same-origin script; an XSS path in the search-results component can exfiltrate them without user interaction.

After successful login, the Express backend returns the JWT as a plain JSON body field. `oauth.component.ts:51` calls `localStorage.setItem("token", ...)` to persist it, and `request.interceptor.ts:13` reads it back and attaches it as an `Authorization` header on every subsequent request. The same storage path applies to TOTP intermediate tokens at `login.component.ts:120`.

**Security assessment**

`localStorage` is readable by any JavaScript running in the same origin. An XSS payload executed via the `bypassSecurityTrustHtml()` call in `search-result.component.ts:143` can extract `localStorage.getItem("token")` and relay the value to an attacker-controlled endpoint in a single line of injected script - no user interaction beyond loading the search page is required.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟠 [F-001 — JWT Session Token Written to localStorage](#f-001) — the session JWT written to `localStorage` at `oauth.component.ts:51` is the primary token exfiltration target; this finding covers both the storage location and the path back to the server.
- 🟠 [F-052 — No Server-Side JWT Revocation Mechanism](#f-052) — the absence of a server-side revocation endpoint at `lib/insecurity.ts:70` means an exfiltrated token from storage cannot be invalidated before it expires.

### 6.4 Authorization Controls


**Systemic weaknesses:** [W-002](#w-002)
**Verdict:** 🟠 Weak

<!-- The line below is mechanically derived from the controls table — LLM must not re-author it. -->
**Controls covered:**

- [6.4.1 Role-Based Access Control](#641-role-based-access-control)
- [6.4.2 Client-Side Route Guards](#642-client-side-route-guards)

**Implemented controls:** `security.isAuthorized()` and `security.isAccounting()` middleware on selected admin-tier API routes, Angular route guards in `frontend/src/app/app.guard.ts` restricting admin and accounting UI sections.

**Assessment:** The server-side authorization boundary has two systemic gaps. First, dozens of sensitive API routes in `server.ts` (lines 310–667) are registered without any authentication middleware, making them callable by unauthenticated users directly. Second, `finale-rest` auto-CRUD at `server.ts:484/501` exposes the full `User` model with no field exclusions, letting a self-registering user include `"role":"admin"` in the POST body and claim admin privilege immediately.

<a id="role-based-access-control"></a>
#### 6.4.1 Role-Based Access Control

**Status:** 🟡 Partial - admin-tier endpoints that do mount `isAuthorized()` middleware enforce the role correctly, but a large subset of routes are registered without any guard, and mass assignment undermines registration integrity.

`security.isAuthorized()` in `lib/insecurity.ts` checks the `role` claim from the verified JWT before allowing access to admin-tier resources. Routes that correctly mount this middleware block non-admin JWT holders as intended.

**Security assessment**

The role boundary is bypassed via two independent paths:

- `server.ts:484/501` registers the `User` resource via `finale-rest` auto-CRUD without excluding the `role` field from write operations. A `POST /api/Users` body containing `"role":"admin"` succeeds - the new account is immediately an admin without any privilege escalation step.
- Multiple resource routes at `server.ts:310`, `server.ts:370`, and many other lines have no authentication middleware at all, so callers with no JWT can invoke them directly.

**Relevant findings**

- 🔴 [F-002 — Missing Authentication Middleware on Sensitive API Routes](#f-002) — missing authentication middleware on sensitive API routes at multiple `server.ts` locations allows unauthenticated callers to invoke privileged operations.
- 🔴 [F-003 — Client-Side-Only Admin and Accounting Role Guard](#f-003) — the client-side-only admin and accounting role guard at `frontend/src/app/app.guard.ts:52` provides no server-side enforcement; these routes are reachable directly without a browser.
- 🔴 [F-013 — Insecure Direct Object Reference](#f-013) — insecure direct object reference across address, basket, order, and payment routes allows one authenticated user to read or modify another user's objects by substituting their own JWT with any target's resource ID.

<a id="client-side-route-guards"></a>
#### 6.4.2 Client-Side Route Guards

**Status:** 🟠 Weak - Angular route guards restrict admin and accounting UI views, but the corresponding API endpoints they protect lack server-side enforcement, so the guards are bypassed by calling the API directly.

`app.guard.ts` implements Angular `CanActivate` guards that decode the JWT from `localStorage` and check the `role` claim before rendering admin or accounting pages. The guards prevent the browser from navigating to restricted pages when the role field is absent or has a non-admin value.

**Security assessment**

Client-side guards protect the rendered UI only. The API endpoints that back those pages must independently enforce authorization server-side; the guards provide no protection against:

- Direct `curl` or `fetch` calls to the same endpoints with a token that lacks the admin role claim - the server does not re-check the claim on routes that lack `isAuthorized()` middleware.
- A forged JWT (possible because the RSA key is committed to the repository) that carries `"role":"admin"` - `app.guard.ts:52` decodes this claim client-side and grants navigation, while the server also accepts the forged token on unguarded routes.

**Relevant findings**

- 🔴 [F-002 — Missing Authentication Middleware on Sensitive API Routes](#f-002) — missing server-side authentication middleware is the direct consequence of relying on client-side guards without corresponding backend enforcement.
- 🔴 [F-003 — Client-Side-Only Admin and Accounting Role Guard](#f-003) — the client-side-only admin guard at `app.guard.ts:52` is the specific guard bypassed by direct API calls or forged tokens.
- 🔴 [F-013 — Insecure Direct Object Reference](#f-013) — IDOR findings demonstrate that even on routes with authentication middleware, authorization at the object level is absent, allowing cross-user data access even when role guards pass.

### 6.5 Query Construction and Data Access Controls


**Systemic weaknesses:** [W-001](#w-001)
**Verdict:** 🟠 Weak

<!-- The line below is mechanically derived from the controls table — LLM must not re-author it. -->
**Controls covered:**

- [6.5.1 ORM Parameterization](#651-orm-parameterization)

**Implemented controls:** Sequelize ORM parameterized finders used across most model-layer data access paths.

**Assessment:** The Sequelize ORM correctly isolates query structure from user-controlled values on most routes. Two SQL injection breakpoints and one NoSQL injection breakpoint exist where raw queries bypass the ORM entirely, covering the login endpoint, the product search endpoint, and the product-review update endpoint.

<a id="orm-parameterization"></a>
#### 6.5.1 ORM Parameterization

**Status:** 🟡 Partial - Sequelize parameterized finders are used throughout the model layer, but login and search routes call `models.sequelize.query()` with concatenated user input, and the review endpoint passes a request-body field into a MarsDB selector without validation.

Sequelize backs all relational data access in this application. The `User`, `Product`, `BasketItem`, and related models use Sequelize finder methods (`findOne`, `findAll`, `where`) that pass user-supplied values as bound parameters by default.

**Security assessment**

The parameterization boundary is broken at three points:

- `routes/login.ts:34` calls `models.sequelize.query()` with `req.body.email` concatenated directly into the SQL string. Submitting `' OR '1'='1` returns the first user row without a matching password.
- `routes/search.ts:23` interpolates the `q` query parameter into a raw SQL `LIKE` clause with the same `models.sequelize.query()` call, allowing SQL injection via the product search field.
- `routes/updateProductReviews.ts:18` passes the `_id` field from the request body directly into a MarsDB selector without casting to `ObjectId`, allowing MongoDB-style operator injection (`{"$gt": ""}`).

**Relevant findings**

- 🔴 [F-010 — SQL Injection via String Concatenation in Login Query](#f-010) — SQL injection at `routes/login.ts:34` allows authentication bypass through raw string concatenation in the login query.
- 🔴 [F-011 — SQL injection via unsanitized query parameter in product search](#f-011) — SQL injection at `routes/search.ts:23` allows data extraction through the product search parameter.
- 🔴 [F-024 — NoSQL injection allows mass review update via unconstrained _id filter](#f-024) — NoSQL injection at `routes/updateProductReviews.ts:18` allows mass review update by supplying an unconstrained `_id` filter.

### 6.6 Input Boundary Validation Controls

**Verdict:** 🟠 Weak

<!-- The line below is mechanically derived from the controls table — LLM must not re-author it. -->
**Controls covered:**

- [6.6.1 Validation Approach](#661-validation-approach)
- [6.6.2 Server-Side Input Validation](#662-server-side-input-validation)

**Implemented controls:** Sequelize model-level validation hooks in `models/user.ts`, `multer` file-size limiting on upload routes.

**Assessment:** Input boundary enforcement is applied inconsistently. The user model conditionally disables sanitization when challenge flags are active, file upload routes accept arbitrary YAML payloads, and the login route has no rate-limiting middleware. No centralized schema-validation layer (e.g. `express-validator` or `zod`) enforces input shapes across routes.

<a id="validation-approach"></a>
#### 6.6.1 Validation Approach

**Status:** 🟠 Weak - input validation is ad hoc per-route with no shared schema layer; model-level sanitization is bypassed by challenge flags, and resource-exhaustion inputs reach parsers without limits.

Most routes accept JSON request bodies parsed by `express.json()`. Validation is applied per-route rather than through a shared schema contract. `models/user.ts` applies sanitization hooks on user data before persistence, and `routes/fileUpload.ts` uses `multer` to limit file sizes on profile photo uploads.

**Security assessment**

Three structural gaps undermine the validation boundary:

- `models/user.ts:48` applies input sanitization conditionally, with the condition gated on a challenge-mode flag. When the flag is set, attacker-controlled values bypass the sanitizer before reaching Sequelize.
- `routes/fileUpload.ts:109` calls `yaml.load()` on the content of an uploaded file without specifying a safe schema. A crafted YAML file containing anchor-alias chains (a "YAML bomb") exhausts server memory.
- No global rate-limiting middleware is applied at the Express router level; individual routes such as the login endpoint at `server.ts:596` must opt into it explicitly, and most do not.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟠 [F-027 — Conditional Input Sanitization Bypass in User Model](#f-027) — conditional input sanitization bypass at `models/user.ts:48` allows attacker-controlled values to persist when the challenge flag is set.
- 🟠 [F-047 — YAML bomb via unrestricted `yaml.load` on uploaded file causes memory exhaustion](#f-047) — `yaml.load()` at `routes/fileUpload.ts:109` without a safe schema allows a YAML bomb to exhaust server memory via a crafted upload.
- 🟠 [F-048 — Missing rate limiting on login endpoint enables credential stuffing and brute-f…](#f-048) — missing rate limiting on the login route at `server.ts:596` enables credential-stuffing and brute-force attacks without any request throttling.

<a id="server-side-input-validation"></a>
#### 6.6.2 Server-Side Input Validation

**Status:** 🟠 Weak - route handlers accept user-controlled values without structural schema validation; type coercion, size limits, and format checks are absent on most endpoints.

`express.json()` parses request bodies and makes them available as `req.body` without type enforcement. Individual routes are responsible for validating field types, ranges, and formats before using them.

**Security assessment**

Server-side validation is sparse and route-specific. Most handlers pass `req.body` fields directly to ORM methods, SQL queries, or file parsers without asserting expected types or ranges. The practical consequence is that every injection and parser-exhaustion finding in this assessment traces back to the same root: a user-controlled value reaching an unsafe sink without being validated or constrained upstream.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟠 [F-027 — Conditional Input Sanitization Bypass in User Model](#f-027) — the sanitization bypass in `models/user.ts:48` is the most concrete failure of server-side input validation at the model layer.
- 🟠 [F-047 — YAML bomb via unrestricted `yaml.load` on uploaded file causes memory exhaustion](#f-047) — the YAML bomb at `routes/fileUpload.ts:109` demonstrates that content-type validation (not just file-size limiting) is absent on upload paths.
- 🟠 [F-048 — Missing rate limiting on login endpoint enables credential stuffing and brute-f…](#f-048) — the absence of rate limiting on `/rest/user/login` at `server.ts:596` is a server-side input boundary control gap that enables volumetric attacks.

### 6.7 Output Encoding and Rendering Controls

**Verdict:** 🟠 Weak

<!-- The line below is mechanically derived from the controls table — LLM must not re-author it. -->
**Controls covered:**

- [6.7.1 Angular Template Security](#671-angular-template-security)

**Implemented controls:** Angular default template escaping via `{{ }}` bindings and `[attribute]` interpolation across most SPA components.

**Assessment:** Angular's default compile-time escaping provides a baseline XSS defense across the majority of the SPA. One deliberate bypass in the search-results component introduces a persistent XSS path that affects any product containing injected markup.

<a id="angular-template-security"></a>
#### 6.7.1 Angular Template Security

**Status:** 🟠 Weak - Angular default escaping is active across most components, but `bypassSecurityTrustHtml()` in the search-results component creates a persistent XSS path for any product description containing injected markup.

Angular's template compiler escapes dynamic content by default across the SPA. Components that bind data with `{{ expression }}` or `[innerHTML]` receive HTML-escaped output unless the developer explicitly bypasses the sanitizer.

**Security assessment**

`search-result.component.ts:143` calls `this.sanitizer.bypassSecurityTrustHtml()` to render product name or description fields as raw HTML. An attacker who can write to the product name or description (via an admin account or via the mass-assignment finding at `server.ts:501`) injects a `<script>` payload that executes in every user's browser when the search results page loads.

**Relevant findings**

- 🔴 [F-029 — Cross-Site Scripting](#f-029) — persistent XSS via `bypassSecurityTrustHtml()` at `search-result.component.ts:143` disables Angular's built-in output encoding on the most frequently visited page in the SPA.

### 6.8 Browser and Cross-Origin Controls

**Verdict:** 🔴 Missing

<!-- The line below is mechanically derived from the controls table — LLM must not re-author it. -->
**Controls covered:**

- [6.8.1 CORS Configuration](#681-cors-configuration)
- [6.8.2 HSTS and Security Headers](#682-hsts-and-security-headers)

**Implemented controls:** `helmet` middleware providing `X-Frame-Options`, `X-Content-Type-Options`, and `Referrer-Policy` headers; `cors()` middleware in `server.ts`.

**Assessment:** Browser hardening is incomplete. Content-Security-Policy is absent entirely, leaving no browser-enforced constraint against inline script execution or exfiltration to unauthorized origins. The Helmet configuration omits both CSP and HSTS. The CORS policy reflects the request `Origin` without a strict allowlist, permitting cross-origin credentialed requests from any origin.

<a id="cors-configuration"></a>
#### 6.8.1 CORS Configuration

**Status:** 🟠 Weak - `cors()` middleware is mounted in `server.ts` but without an explicit origin allowlist, reflecting the caller's `Origin` header for all API routes.

`server.ts` mounts `app.use(cors())` globally, enabling cross-origin requests from any origin. The default `cors()` configuration with no options reflects the requesting `Origin` in the `Access-Control-Allow-Origin` response header and permits `credentials: true` cross-origin requests.

**Security assessment**

A wildcard-permissive CORS policy allows any web page to make authenticated cross-origin requests to API endpoints and read the responses. Combined with JWT tokens stored in `localStorage` (readable by cross-origin scripts that exploit CORS), an attacker-controlled page can proxy authenticated API calls on behalf of a logged-in victim. The combination of permissive CORS and localStorage token storage (rather than `HttpOnly` cookies) amplifies the impact of both.

**Relevant findings**

- No dedicated finding routed in this assessment.

<a id="hsts-and-security-headers"></a>
#### 6.8.2 HSTS and Security Headers

**Status:** 🟡 Partial - Helmet supplies several security headers, but HSTS and Content-Security-Policy are both absent, leaving the application without browser-enforced transport and script-execution policies.

`helmet` is mounted in `server.ts` and applies a default set of HTTP security response headers to all responses. These include `X-Frame-Options: SAMEORIGIN`, `X-Content-Type-Options: nosniff`, and `Referrer-Policy`.

**Security assessment**

Two critical browser policy headers are absent:

- Content-Security-Policy is not configured anywhere in the Express middleware stack. Without CSP, the browser places no restriction on inline script execution or on which origins the page may contact, leaving no browser-side mitigation for the XSS path in `search-result.component.ts:143`.
- HSTS is not sent. The application does not enforce HTTPS via the `Strict-Transport-Security` header, allowing sessions to be initiated over plaintext HTTP if the deployment serves both.

**Relevant findings**

- No dedicated finding routed in this assessment.

_Additional cataloged controls without a dedicated subsection (no implementation prose and no linked findings): Content Security Policy._

### 6.9 Cryptography Secrets and Data Protection


**Systemic weaknesses:** [W-003](#w-003), [W-007](#w-007)
**Verdict:** 🟠 Weak

<!-- The line below is mechanically derived from the controls table — LLM must not re-author it. -->
**Controls covered:**

- [6.9.1 Password Hashing](#691-password-hashing)
- [6.9.2 Secret and Key Management](#692-secret-and-key-management)

**Implemented controls:** Password hashing applied in the `models/user.ts` setter before storage, `RS256` algorithm chosen for JWT signing in `lib/insecurity.ts`.

**Assessment:** The cryptographic boundary is undermined by hardcoded key material and a broken hash primitive. `lib/insecurity.ts` embeds the RSA private key, an HMAC secret, and auxiliary secrets as string literals - any repository clone has full signing capability. The password hash function in `models/user.ts:76` uses unsalted `MD5`, making any database dump immediately recoverable. `RS256` is a reasonable algorithm choice, but the key is committed to the repository, defeating the algorithm selection.

<a id="password-hashing"></a>
#### 6.9.1 Password Hashing

**Status:** 🟢 Adequate - a hash function is applied to passwords before storage; the hash primitive and key material in adjacent crypto operations present greater risk than the hashing step itself.

`models/user.ts` applies a hash function in the password setter before writing to the `Users` table. The operation runs at account creation and on every password change, ensuring that plaintext passwords are never stored directly.

**Security assessment**

The hashing step is present, but the surrounding cryptographic infrastructure has critical weaknesses that affect the same authentication boundary:

- `lib/insecurity.ts:21` stores the RSA private key as a hardcoded string literal. Any attacker who clones the repository can sign JWTs for any user, bypassing the need to crack a password hash entirely.
- `lib/insecurity.ts:52` accepts `alg:none` in the JWT header, allowing token forgery without using the key at all.
- `oauth.component.ts:30` derives local account passwords from the email claim using a deterministic algorithm exposed in the repository, creating predictable credentials for OAuth-linked accounts.

**Relevant findings**

- 🔴 [F-004 — RSA Private Key Hardcoded as String Literal](#f-004) — the hardcoded RSA private key at `lib/insecurity.ts:21` is the primary signing material for the session tokens issued after password verification, making key compromise more consequential than hash cracking.
- 🔴 [F-005 — JWT Algorithm Restriction Missing express-jwt Accepts algorithm none](#f-005) — the absent algorithm allowlist in `express-jwt` at `lib/insecurity.ts:52` allows algorithm confusion that defeats the RS256 signing protecting sessions established through password login.
- 🔴 [F-006 — OAuth Implicit Flow with Algorithmically Derived Password](#f-006) — the OAuth flow at `oauth.component.ts:30` derives a local password from the email claim algorithmically, creating credentials predictable from public data without any password hashing applied to the OAuth path.

<a id="secret-and-key-management"></a><a id="secret-management"></a>
#### 6.9.2 Secret and Key Management

**Status:** 🟡 Partial - multiple secrets are present in the codebase (RSA key pair, HMAC secret, BIP-39 mnemonic), all hardcoded as string literals in source files that are committed to a public repository.

`lib/insecurity.ts` is the central secret-holding file for this application. It exports the RSA private and public keys used for JWT signing, an HMAC secret used to hash security-question answers, and auxiliary security material. `routes/checkKeys.ts` holds the BIP-39 mnemonic for the Web3 wallet surface.

**Security assessment**

Every production-significant secret in this application is committed to source control as a string literal:

- `lib/insecurity.ts:21` holds the RSA private key - repository-public, so any fork or CI build log can sign arbitrary JWTs.
- `lib/insecurity.ts:42` holds the HMAC secret used to hash security-question answers. Any attacker with the secret can pre-compute hashes of OSINT-derived answers offline without querying the application.
- `lib/insecurity.ts:53` uses a non-cryptographic RNG (`Math.random()`) for values that should be cryptographically random ([CWE-330](https://cwe.mitre.org/data/definitions/330.html)).

**Relevant findings**

- 🔴 [F-004 — RSA Private Key Hardcoded as String Literal](#f-004) — the hardcoded RSA private key at `lib/insecurity.ts:21` gives any repository reader the ability to sign valid session tokens without authentication.
- 🔴 [F-005 — JWT Algorithm Restriction Missing express-jwt Accepts algorithm none](#f-005) — the JWT algorithm restriction gap at `lib/insecurity.ts:52` makes the hardcoded key less relevant for attackers — they can forge tokens without a key by specifying `alg:none`.
- 🔴 [F-006 — OAuth Implicit Flow with Algorithmically Derived Password](#f-006) — the deterministic password derived from an OAuth email claim at `oauth.component.ts:30` is a secret-management failure: the derivation function is exposed in the repository, making OAuth credentials predictable.

### 6.10 File Parser and Outbound Request Controls


**Systemic weaknesses:** [W-005](#w-005)
**Verdict:** 🟠 Weak

<!-- The line below is mechanically derived from the controls table — LLM must not re-author it. -->
**Controls covered:**

- [6.10.1 File Upload Security](#6101-file-upload-security)
- [6.10.2 XML / YAML Parsing](#6102-xml--yaml-parsing)
- [6.10.3 Outbound Request Control](#6103-outbound-request-control)

**Implemented controls:** `multer` file-size limits on profile image upload, `express-fileupload` on the complaint endpoint.

**Assessment:** Parser and outbound controls have three distinct exploitation paths: the B2B order endpoint evaluates user-supplied JavaScript in a `notevil` sandbox that is escapable; file uploads accept YAML content with unsafe `yaml.load()`; and outbound HTTP requests in the profile-image-URL endpoint are not restricted by scheme or host. The open-redirect handler accepts substring-matched allowlist entries, enabling redirect to arbitrary external URLs.

<a id="file-upload-security"></a>
#### 6.10.1 File Upload Security

**Status:** 🟠 Weak - `multer` imposes a file-size cap on the profile-image route but performs no content-type or magic-byte validation; files accepted via the complaint and FTP routes undergo no server-side content inspection before storage.

File uploads are handled in two contexts: profile image uploads via `routes/profileImageUpload.ts` (using `multer` with a file-size cap) and B2B order file submission via `routes/fileUpload.ts`. The FTP directory (`ftp/`) serves as a file store accessible from the application.

**Security assessment**

- `routes/b2bOrder.ts:21` passes user-supplied JavaScript expressions to `notevil` for evaluation. The `notevil` sandbox is escapable, providing a remote code execution path without any uploaded file involved - the input is a POST body field, not a file.
- `ftp/` stores uploaded files without content validation; files placed there through the complaint or profile-upload routes are served statically.
- The path-traversal finding at `routes/dataErasure.ts:104` allows an authenticated user to read files outside the intended erasure directory by supplying a traversal sequence in the request parameter.

**Relevant findings**

- 🔴 [F-012 — Remote code execution via notevil sandbox escape in B2B order endpoint](#f-012) — RCE via `notevil` sandbox escape at `routes/b2bOrder.ts:21` is the highest-severity consequence of accepting user-supplied expressions in the B2B order parser.
- 🟠 [F-021 — Open Redirect via Substring Allowlist Bypass](#f-021) — open redirect at `routes/redirect.ts:17` via substring allowlist bypass allows phishing redirects by appending a URL containing the allowed substring as a path component.
- 🟠 [F-022 — Path traversal filesystem access from request input](#f-022) — path traversal at `routes/dataErasure.ts:104` reads files from the server filesystem outside the intended erasure scope when the request input contains `../` sequences.

<a id="xml-yaml-parsing"></a>
#### 6.10.2 XML / YAML Parsing

**Status:** 🟠 Weak - `yaml.load()` is called without a safe schema on uploaded files, and no XML parser hardening (external-entity disabling) has been verified in the complaint-upload path.

`routes/fileUpload.ts:109` reads uploaded file content and passes it to `yaml.load()`. YAML parsing with the default full loader allows alias-heavy documents ("YAML bombs") to exhaust server memory and processes external entities or executable tags in some YAML implementations.

**Security assessment**

`routes/fileUpload.ts:109` calls `yaml.load()` without `{ schema: FAILSAFE_SCHEMA }` or `yaml.safeLoad()`. A crafted YAML document with deeply nested anchor-alias chains causes quadratic or exponential memory growth, exhausting heap space and crashing the Node\.js process (denial of service).

**Relevant findings**

- 🔴 [F-012 — Remote code execution via notevil sandbox escape in B2B order endpoint](#f-012) — the `notevil` sandbox escape is the sister parser-execution finding to the YAML bomb: both accept user-supplied content and evaluate or parse it without safe-mode restrictions.
- 🟠 [F-021 — Open Redirect via Substring Allowlist Bypass](#f-021) — the open-redirect substring bypass shares the same root as YAML parsing: user-supplied content is passed to a function that trusts structural properties of the input without sufficient validation.
- 🟠 [F-022 — Path traversal filesystem access from request input](#f-022) — path traversal is the filesystem-layer analog of parser exploitation — both allow user input to navigate beyond the intended scope of an operation.

<a id="outbound-request-control"></a><a id="outbound-request-control-ssrf"></a>
#### 6.10.3 Outbound Request Control

**Status:** 🟠 Weak - `routes/profileImageUrlUpload.ts` fetches a user-supplied URL without scheme or host validation, enabling SSRF to internal network addresses including cloud metadata endpoints.

`routes/profileImageUrlUpload.ts:24` accepts a `imageUrl` parameter from the request body and performs an outbound HTTP fetch to that URL before storing the response as the user's profile image. No allowlist or blocklist of schemes or hosts is applied before the fetch.

**Security assessment**

An attacker submitting `http://169.254.169.254/latest/meta-data/` or an internal service URL as the `imageUrl` causes the Express backend to make an outbound request to that address and return the response body as if it were an image. This reveals cloud instance metadata, internal service responses, or intranet resources not reachable from the attacker's network location.

**Relevant findings**

- 🔴 [F-012 — Remote code execution via notevil sandbox escape in B2B order endpoint](#f-012) — the `notevil` sandbox RCE provides server-side code execution that would allow an attacker to make arbitrary outbound requests independently of the profile-image URL path.
- 🟠 [F-021 — Open Redirect via Substring Allowlist Bypass](#f-021) — the open-redirect finding at `routes/redirect.ts:17` is the client-side analog: user-supplied URLs are followed without strict validation.
- 🟠 [F-022 — Path traversal filesystem access from request input](#f-022) — path traversal at `routes/dataErasure.ts:104` is the filesystem-layer equivalent: unvalidated user input directs a server-side operation to an unintended target.

### 6.11 Operations Runtime and Supply Chain Controls


**Systemic weaknesses:** [W-006](#w-006)
**Verdict:** 🔴 Missing

<!-- The line below is mechanically derived from the controls table — LLM must not re-author it. -->
**Controls covered:**

- [6.11.1 Container Security](#6111-container-security)
- [6.11.2 CI/CD Supply Chain Security](#6112-cicd-supply-chain-security)
- [6.11.3 Automated SCA scanning](#6113-automated-sca-scanning)
- [6.11.4 Automated dependency updates](#6114-automated-dependency-updates)
- [6.11.5 Lockfile hygiene](#6115-lockfile-hygiene)

**Implemented controls:** GitHub Actions workflows for CI, CodeQL static analysis on push, `npm audit` available in the toolchain.

**Assessment:** Supply-chain posture is split: automated vulnerability scanning is present (CodeQL, npm audit capability) but automated dependency update tooling and lockfile integrity enforcement are absent. CI workflows run without workflow-level permissions blocks, `GITHUB_TOKEN` retains default write scope, third-party actions are pinned to floating version tags rather than commit SHAs in several workflows, and the Dockerfile uses `npm install` (which executes `postinstall` hooks from any installed package) rather than `npm ci --ignore-scripts`.

<a id="container-security"></a>
#### 6.11.1 Container Security

**Status:** 🟡 Partial - a multi-stage Dockerfile is present and produces a reduced-surface image, but the base image is not digest-pinned, the container runs as root, and `npm install` runs `postinstall` hooks from dependencies.

`Dockerfile` and `test/smoke/Dockerfile` define the container build process. The production image copies application files and runs `node` to start the Express server. The base image is specified by floating tag rather than digest.

**Security assessment**

Three container-level hardening gaps reduce supply-chain integrity:

- `Dockerfile:1` specifies the base image by floating tag, so the build may pull a different (potentially compromised) image on each run without the tag changing.
- `test/smoke/Dockerfile` runs the container process as root (no `USER` directive at `Dockerfile:1`), violating the principle of least privilege for container processes.
- `Dockerfile:4` runs `npm install` rather than `npm ci --ignore-scripts`; any dependency in `package.json` with a `postinstall` script executes arbitrary code during the Docker build.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟠 [F-017 — Express-jwt 0.1.3 Critically Outdated JWT Middleware](#f-017) — `express-jwt@0.1.3` at `lib/insecurity.ts:10` is a critically outdated dependency; the absence of automated updates means vulnerable packages persist until manual intervention.
- 🟠 [F-025 — Floating Tag on Third-Party CI Action](#f-025) — floating tag on third-party CI actions at `.github/workflows/image_actions.yml:33` allows the action to be substituted with a compromised version without a tag change.
- 🟠 [F-026 — Postinstall Hook Executes Frontend Build on npm install](#f-026) — `postinstall` hook execution at `package.json:56` runs arbitrary scripts from dependencies during `npm install`, including inside Docker builds.

<a id="cicd-supply-chain-security"></a>
#### 6.11.2 CI/CD Supply Chain Security

**Status:** 🟡 Partial - GitHub Actions workflows are present and run automated tests, but workflow-level permission blocks are absent from most workflow files, third-party actions are not consistently pinned to commit SHAs, and `pull_request_target` exposes a privileged token to untrusted code.

GitHub Actions automates CI, release packaging, and image publishing. Workflows in `.github/workflows/` include `ci.yml`, `release.yml`, `codeql-analysis.yml`, and several maintenance workflows.

**Security assessment**

Four workflow-level supply-chain weaknesses exist:

- Thirteen workflow files (including `ci.yml`, `release.yml`, and others) have no top-level `permissions:` block, so they run with the default `GITHUB_TOKEN` write scope for all permission categories - a compromised step in any of these workflows can write to the repository or packages.
- `.github/workflows/image_actions.yml:33` and `.github/workflows/codeql-analysis.yml:24` pin third-party actions to floating version tags; a maintainer can push a new tag version containing malicious code.
- `.github/workflows/pr-compliance.yml:438` uses `pull_request_target` with `org_admin_token` in scope, giving code from pull-request branches access to organization-level secrets.
- `package.json:56` defines a `postinstall` script that triggers a frontend build whenever any `npm install` runs, including in CI and Docker build contexts.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟠 [F-017 — Express-jwt 0.1.3 Critically Outdated JWT Middleware](#f-017) — `express-jwt@0.1.3` is critically outdated; the absence of CI SCA gating means this version was not flagged and blocked before reaching production.
- 🟠 [F-025 — Floating Tag on Third-Party CI Action](#f-025) — floating tag on the `calibreapp/image-actions` third-party action at `.github/workflows/image_actions.yml:33` allows tag-based substitution attacks.
- 🟠 [F-026 — Postinstall Hook Executes Frontend Build on npm install](#f-026) — the `postinstall` hook at `package.json:56` runs untrusted build scripts in CI without `--ignore-scripts` isolation.

<a id="automated-sca-scanning"></a>
#### 6.11.3 Automated SCA scanning

**Status:** 🟢 Adequate - CodeQL analysis and `npm audit` capability are present in the CI pipeline; the codebase is scanned for known-vulnerable dependencies on each push.

`.github/workflows/codeql-analysis.yml` runs GitHub's CodeQL static analysis engine on the TypeScript codebase on each push to the main branch. `npm audit` is available in the toolchain and can be invoked manually or in CI steps.

**Security assessment**

SCA tooling is present and runs automatically. The primary gap is that `npm audit` results are not enforced as a CI gate - failing audits do not block merges. the dependency scanner flags vulnerabilities after they reach the repository; without automated update tooling ([§6.11.4](#6114-automated-dependency-updates)), findings from the scanner do not trigger remediation without manual intervention.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟠 [F-017 — Express-jwt 0.1.3 Critically Outdated JWT Middleware](#f-017) — `express-jwt@0.1.3` is critically outdated; the SCA tooling would flag this but the absence of an audit gate and automated updates means it persists.
- 🟠 [F-025 — Floating Tag on Third-Party CI Action](#f-025) — the floating-tag CI action weakness is outside the scope of npm SCA scanning (it is a workflow supply-chain issue, not a package vulnerability).
- 🟠 [F-026 — Postinstall Hook Executes Frontend Build on npm install](#f-026) — the `postinstall` hook execution risk is also not detected by version-based SCA; it requires workflow hardening (`--ignore-scripts`) rather than a dependency update.

<a id="automated-dependency-updates"></a>
#### 6.11.4 Automated dependency updates

**Status:** 🔴 Missing - no Dependabot, Renovate, or equivalent automated PR tooling is configured; dependency updates are entirely manual.

No `.github/dependabot.yml` or `renovate.json` configuration file is present in the repository. There is no automated tooling that monitors `package.json` or `.github/workflows/*.yml` for outdated dependency or action pins and opens pull requests to update them.

**Security assessment**

Without automated update PRs, package versions and action pins accumulate drift over time. `express-jwt@0.1.3` is the most acute example: this version accepts `alg:none` in JWT headers, a critical vulnerability that has been publicly known for years. Automated tooling would have proposed an upgrade to `express-jwt@8.x` (which pins algorithms by default) far earlier. The same gap affects the floating-tag action pins identified in CI/CD supply-chain findings.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟠 [F-017 — Express-jwt 0.1.3 Critically Outdated JWT Middleware](#f-017) — `express-jwt@0.1.3` at `lib/insecurity.ts:10` is the primary example of a vulnerability that automated dependency updates would have remediated.
- 🟠 [F-025 — Floating Tag on Third-Party CI Action](#f-025) — floating tag pins on third-party CI actions are also a form of dependency drift that automated tooling (Dependabot for Actions) can remediate.
- 🟠 [F-026 — Postinstall Hook Executes Frontend Build on npm install](#f-026) — the `postinstall` hook is a dependency-hygiene issue that a lockfile + `--ignore-scripts` CI policy would contain.

<a id="lockfile-hygiene"></a>
#### 6.11.5 Lockfile hygiene

**Status:** 🔴 Missing - `package-lock.json` is not committed with `--package-lock-only` enforcement; `npm install` runs with scripts enabled in CI and Docker, allowing resolution-time supply-chain injection.

The repository includes `package.json` and `package-lock.json`, but CI builds run `npm install` rather than `npm ci`. `npm install` may update the lockfile and resolves floating ranges at install time, weakening the reproducibility guarantee that `npm ci` provides.

**Security assessment**

Running `npm install` instead of `npm ci --ignore-scripts` in CI and Docker builds introduces two risks:

- The lockfile is not enforced as the sole source of dependency versions; a tampered registry entry for a floating semver range (`^`) can substitute a different version between pipeline runs.
- `postinstall` scripts from any dependency run automatically, including scripts from transitively introduced packages, without explicit review.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟠 [F-017 — Express-jwt 0.1.3 Critically Outdated JWT Middleware](#f-017) — `express-jwt@0.1.3` illustrates what persists when lockfile hygiene and automated updates are both absent.
- 🟠 [F-025 — Floating Tag on Third-Party CI Action](#f-025) — floating CI action tags are the workflow-layer analog of floating package ranges — both allow the resolved artifact to differ from what was reviewed.
- 🟠 [F-026 — Postinstall Hook Executes Frontend Build on npm install](#f-026) — the `postinstall` hook at `package.json:56` executes because `npm install` (not `npm ci --ignore-scripts`) is used; lockfile enforcement alone does not suppress hook execution.

### 6.12 Real-time and Not Applicable Controls

<!-- §6.12 LOCKED — mechanically derived from absence of real-time findings. Renderer must not rewrite the line below. -->
_Not applicable - no real-time / WebSocket findings routed to this category, and no AI/LLM, GraphQL, or gRPC surfaces detected by the recon scan. Controls catalogued elsewhere (container hardening, dependency determinism) are covered in their primary [§6](#6-security-architecture) sections._

### 6.13 Defense-in-Depth Summary

**Verdict:** -

`RS256` is a sound algorithm choice for JWT signing, and Helm provides baseline response-header hardening (`X-Frame-Options`, `X-Content-Type-Options`, `Referrer-Policy`). Sequelize ORM parameterization covers most data-access paths correctly. CodeQL static analysis runs automatically on CI pushes. The multi-stage Dockerfile reduces the production image surface relative to a plain `node` base. These represent the positive controls that function as designed and should be preserved in any remediation effort.

The layered defense that would contain the dominant attack paths requires repairs at five control boundaries. Replacing raw SQL concatenation in `routes/login.ts:34` and `routes/search.ts:23` with Sequelize parameterized queries closes the authentication bypass and data-extraction paths. Moving the RSA private key, HMAC secret, and auxiliary secrets in `lib/insecurity.ts` to runtime environment variables (or a secrets manager) eliminates the ability to forge session tokens from a repository clone. Pinning the JWT algorithm allowlist to `RS256` in `express-jwt` closes the `alg:none` bypass. Adding a Content-Security-Policy header removes the browser's permissiveness toward inline script execution triggered by the XSS finding at `search-result.component.ts:143`. Switching CI and Docker builds from `npm install` to `npm ci --ignore-scripts` and adding a workflow-level `permissions: { contents: read }` block to all GitHub Actions workflows closes the two highest-impact supply-chain gaps.

<!-- enriched:standard -->

---

<a id="weakness-register"></a>
## 7. Weakness Register

Systemic control gaps behind the findings, ordered by severity (W-001 = most severe). Each weakness names the missing, home-grown, or misused control, the findings that evidence it, the components it spans, and its remediation. A weakness may also rest on observed unsafe practice or an absent architectural control with no confirmed exploit - only confirmed findings carry a CVSS score.

- 🔴 **Critical** · [W-001](#w-001) - Database access relies on concatenated queries · confirmed · 2 findings · 2 components
- 🔴 **Critical** · [W-002](#w-002) - Authorization is implemented route by route · confirmed · 3 findings · 2 components
- 🔴 **Critical** · [W-003](#w-003) - Secrets are committed to source instead of a managed store · confirmed · 4 findings · 3 components
- 🟠 **High** · [W-004](#w-004) - Endpoints are reachable without enforced authentication · confirmed · 2 findings · 2 components
- 🟠 **High** · [W-005](#w-005) - Input handling lacks enforced boundary validation · confirmed · 1 finding · 1 component
- 🟠 **High** · [W-006](#w-006) - Build pipeline trusts mutable third-party references · confirmed · 5 findings · 1 component
- 🟡 **Medium** · [W-007](#w-007) - Security-sensitive data uses weak cryptographic primitives · observed-practice · 4 findings · 3 components
- 🟡 **Medium** · [W-008](#w-008) - Frontend rendering lacks enforced output encoding · design-risk · 0 findings · 0 components

<a id="w-001"></a>
### W-001 — Database access relies on concatenated queries

🔴 **Critical** · design weakness · confirmed · 2 findings

Database queries are assembled from application values instead of passing those values through an enforced parameterised data-access path. This leaves every call site responsible for preserving query structure.

**Architectural anti-pattern - Raw SQL string interpolation.** Login and product-search queries are built by directly concatenating user-supplied values into query strings; parameterized statements are absent, making database injection an intrinsic property of the current data-access layer rather than an isolated edge case.

**Confirmed findings:**

- 🔴 [F-010](#f-010) — SQL Injection via String Concatenation in Login Query (`routes/login.ts:34`)
- 🔴 [F-011](#f-011) — SQL injection via unsanitized query parameter in product search (`search.ts:23`)

**Architecture evidence:** Parameterized Queries, ORM / Repository Layer

**Affected components:** [C-03](#c-03), [C-02](#c-02)

**Remediation:**

- **Structural** — provide one parameterised repository or query-builder path and prohibit application-value interpolation in database queries
- **Tactical** — ● [M-010](#m-010) — Replace raw SQL string concatenation with Sequelize parameterised query or ORM finder, ● [M-011](#m-011) — Parameterize the product search LIKE query

<a id="w-002"></a>
### W-002 — Authorization is implemented route by route

🔴 **Critical** · design weakness · confirmed · 3 findings

Authorization depends on per-handler checks instead of a policy boundary that consistently enforces role, ownership, and tenant scope. New routes can bypass protection by omitting a local check.

**Architectural anti-pattern - Missing server-side authorization layer.** Role and ownership enforcement exists only in the frontend; the server accepts role-change requests and object ownership transfers from any authenticated caller without verifying identity, making privilege escalation trivially reproducible from any user account.

**Confirmed findings:**

- 🔴 [F-002](#f-002) — Missing Authentication Middleware on Sensitive API Routes
- 🔴 [F-013](#f-013) — Insecure Direct Object Reference
- 🔴 [F-053](#f-053) — Discount Cap Unenforced at Server Layer in generateCoupon Tool (`chat.ts:179`)

**Architecture evidence:** Centralised AuthZ Policy, Role / Scope Enforcement, Ownership Check, Object-Level Ownership Check, Tenant Scoping

**Affected components:** [C-02](#c-02), [C-04](#c-04)

**Remediation:**

- **Structural** — enforce authorization through a shared server-side policy layer and make ownership and tenant scope mandatory inputs to data access
- **Tactical** — ◕ [M-002](#m-002) — Enforce security response headers policy (CSP, HSTS, X-Frame-Options), ● [M-013](#m-013) — Replace req.body.UserId/userId/ownerId with req.user.id (or equivalent session-derived identity) in every WHERE/filter clause., ◕ [M-053](#m-053) — Add server-side discount cap and Zod max constraint to generateCoupon tool input

<a id="w-003"></a>
### W-003 — Secrets are committed to source instead of a managed store

🔴 **Critical** · design weakness · confirmed · 4 findings

Cryptographic keys, credentials, and other high-entropy secrets are embedded as literals in source or config rather than resolved at runtime from a managed secret store, so anyone with repository read access obtains reusable signing material and credentials.

**Architectural anti-pattern - Secrets hardcoded in source.** Cryptographic signing keys and wallet mnemonics are embedded as string literals in source files; any person with repository access can extract them without special tooling, and rotation requires a code change and full redeployment.

**Confirmed findings:**

- 🔴 [F-004](#f-004) — RSA Private Key Hardcoded as String Literal (`lib/insecurity.ts:21`)
- 🔴 [F-006](#f-006) — OAuth Implicit Flow with Algorithmically Derived Password
- 🔴 [F-035](#f-035) — Hardcoded HMAC secret exposes security-answer hashes (`lib/insecurity.ts:42`)
- 🔴 [F-045](#f-045) — Hardcoded BIP-39 Mnemonic Exposes Ethereum Private Key (`routes/checkKeys.ts:10`)

**Architecture evidence:** Managed Secret Store, Runtime Secret Injection

**Affected components:** [C-03](#c-03), [C-02](#c-02), [C-08](#c-08)

**Remediation:**

- **Structural** — move every secret to a managed secret store or injected environment configuration, rotate the exposed values, and add secret-scanning to CI
- **Tactical** — ● [M-004](#m-004) — Load RSA private key from an environment variable or secret manager at startup, ● [M-006](#m-006) — Migrate OAuth flow to authorization code + PKCE and decouple OAuth identity from password-based auth, ◕ [M-035](#m-035) — Replace hardcoded HMAC secret with environment-variable key and add per-answer salt, ◕ [M-045](#m-045) — Move Ethereum wallet mnemonic to an environment variable or secrets manager and remove the hardcoded literal

<a id="w-004"></a>
### W-004 — Endpoints are reachable without enforced authentication

🟠 **High** · design weakness · confirmed · 2 findings

Sensitive API routes and real-time channels are exposed without an enforced authentication check at the endpoint boundary. Access control depends on each handler (or the caller) remembering to require a session, so an unauthenticated client can reach privileged operations directly.

**Confirmed findings:**

- 🔴 [F-020](#f-020) — Unauthenticated WebSocket Channel
- 🔴 [F-054](#f-054) — All Web3/NFT Endpoints Lack Authentication Middleware (`routes/checkKeys.ts:7`)

**Architecture evidence:** Route Authentication Middleware, Server-Side Session Enforcement

**Affected components:** [C-07](#c-07), [C-08](#c-08)

**Remediation:**

- **Structural** — enforce authentication centrally at the routing and channel boundary so every exposed endpoint requires a verified session unless explicitly marked public
- **Tactical** — ◕ [M-020](#m-020) — Add socket.io authentication middleware to validate session or JWT on every connection, ◕ [M-054](#m-054) — Add `security.isAuthorized()` middleware to all web3/NFT route registrations

<a id="w-005"></a>
### W-005 — Input handling lacks enforced boundary validation

🟠 **High** · design weakness · confirmed · 1 finding

Request handlers do not validate input against one enforced server-side schema or allowlist at the boundary - validation is either absent on the vulnerable parameters or limited to rejecting selected bad patterns (a blacklist). New encodings and unanticipated input forms can therefore reach downstream parsers or interpreters.

**Confirmed findings:**

- 🟠 [F-022](#f-022) — Path traversal filesystem access from request input `routes/dataErasure.ts:104`

**Architecture evidence:** Schema Validation, Allowlist Validation

**Affected components:** [C-02](#c-02)

**Remediation:**

- **Structural** — enforce server-side schemas and domain-specific allowlists before input reaches parsing, persistence, or command construction
- **Tactical** — ◕ [M-022](#m-022) — Resolve the path, assert it stays within an allowed base directory (path.resolve + startsWith), and use path.basename on user input.

<a id="w-006"></a>
### W-006 — Build pipeline trusts mutable third-party references

🟠 **High** · design weakness · confirmed · 5 findings

CI/CD workflows resolve third-party actions and other build dependencies to mutable tags or branches instead of immutable commit digests, so a retagged or compromised upstream runs inside the pipeline with its token and secret scope.

**Confirmed findings:**

- 🟠 [F-025](#f-025) — Floating Tag on Third-Party CI Action (`image_actions.yml:33`)
- 🟠 [F-026](#f-026) — Postinstall Hook Executes Frontend Build on npm install (`.json:56`)
- 🟠 [F-038](#f-038) — Third-party GitHub Actions pinned to commit SHA
- 🟡 [F-067](#f-067) — Pull_request_target Workflow Exposes `ORG_ADMIN_TOKEN` to Privileged Runner
- [F-068](#f-068)

**Architecture evidence:** Commit-SHA Action Pinning, Dependency Provenance Verification

**Affected components:** [C-06](#c-06)

**Remediation:**

- **Structural** — pin every third-party action and build dependency to an immutable commit SHA (or a vetted internal mirror) and enforce SHA-pinning in CI
- **Tactical** — ◕ [M-025](#m-025) — Pin calibreapp/image-actions to a full commit SHA in `image_actions.yml`, ◕ [M-026](#m-026) — Replace postinstall with an explicit CI build step and add --ignore-scripts to the nested frontend install, ◕ [M-038](#m-038) — Pin every third-party action to a full 40-char commit SHA, ◑ [M-067](#m-067) — Scope pull_request_target secrets to the minimum required and isolate ORG_ADMIN_TOKEN to a separate workflow

<a id="w-007"></a>
### W-007 — Security-sensitive data uses weak cryptographic primitives

🟡 **Medium** · implementation weakness · observed-practice · 4 findings

Password, token, or integrity protection uses a weak hash, predictable random source, or insufficient work factor. The application may use a standard library, but the selected primitive does not provide the required security property.

**Practice sites:**

- 🔴 [F-005](#f-005) — JWT Algorithm Restriction Missing express-jwt Accepts algorithm none (`lib/insecurity.ts:52`)
- 🔴 [F-008](#f-008) — Unsalted MD5 Password Hashing (`models/user.ts:76`) (`models/user.ts:76`)
- 🟠 [F-023](#f-023) — Non-cryptographic RNG for a secret/token `lib/insecurity.ts:53` (`lib/insecurity.ts:53`)
- 🟡 [F-055](#f-055) — Broken hash primitive `lib/insecurity.ts:41` (MD5/SHA-1) (`lib/insecurity.ts:41`)

**Affected components:** [C-03](#c-03), [C-02](#c-02), [C-05](#c-05)

**Remediation:**

- **Structural** — standardise on a password KDF, a CSPRNG for secrets, and modern authenticated cryptographic primitives with centrally reviewed parameters
- **Tactical** — ● [M-005](#m-005) — Pin allowed algorithms to RS256 in expressJwt and jwt.verify calls, ● [M-008](#m-008) — Replace MD5 with bcrypt (cost factor ≥12) in User model password setter, ◕ [M-023](#m-023) — Use crypto.randomBytes / crypto.randomUUID / webcrypto getRandomValues for any security value., ◑ [M-055](#m-055) — Use SHA-256+ for integrity and a password KDF (argon2id / bcrypt / scrypt) for credentials.

<a id="w-008"></a>
### W-008 — Frontend rendering lacks enforced output encoding

🟡 **Medium** · design weakness · design-risk

Browser rendering paths write HTML or DOM content directly without one enforced contextual-encoding or sanitisation boundary. Safety depends on each individual component using the right API.

**Architecture evidence:** Template Autoescape, Output Encoding, Content Security Policy

**Remediation:**

- **Structural** — use framework-safe rendering by default and isolate any required raw HTML behind one reviewed sanitisation and Trusted Types policy

---

## 8. Findings Register

Findings are grouped by severity (Critical → High → Medium → Low); within a tier they are ordered by attack vektor (Repo-Read → Internet-Anon → Internet-User → Victim-Required). Each finding is a card with the same fixed fields, in order: **Severity · Component · Location** → **Issue** → **Root cause** → **Evidence** → **Fix** → **Classification** (with external CWE / OWASP links).

**Risk Distribution:** 🔴 Critical: 12 · 🟠 High: 43 · 🟡 Medium: 13 · 🟢 Low: 0 · **Total findings: 68**
**STRIDE Coverage:** Spoofing: 12 · Tampering: 16 · Repudiation: 2 · Information Disclosure: 21 · Denial of Service: 7 · Elevation of Privilege: 10

The systemic root-cause view is summarized in **Top Weaknesses** in the Management Summary; evidence-backed weaknesses are documented in the [Weakness Register](#weakness-register).
